The factualist (possible worlds) analysis makes counterfactuals incoherent.

167. [EP-139] Counterfactuals are crypto-probability propositions
   Sources: WORK-037
   Thesis: 'If A had been the case, then B would have been the case' means: The proposition K and A gives a high probability to B, where K is the conjunction of propositions we know to be true, minus the proposition that A and B were not the case.

168. [EP-140] Probability is epistemic not metaphysical
   Sources: WORK-037
   Thesis: Probability is an epistemic, not a metaphysical, notion. Probability is a logical relation between propositions, not a property of states of affairs. When we say 'there is a 5% probability that Socrates had four kidneys,' we are saying something about epistemic possibility given our data, not about metaphysical possibility.

169. [EP-141] False propositions probabilify other propositions
   Sources: WORK-037
   Thesis: False propositions probabilify other propositions just like true ones. 'The moon is made of cheese' is false, but it still gives very high probability to 'there is a piece of cheese that weighs more than 100 lbs.' 'Kennedy didn't die in '63' is false but still gives probability to other propositions.

170. [EP-142] Counterfactual truth independent of empirical discoveries
   Sources: WORK-037
   Thesis: The truth of counterfactuals is not contingent on empirical discoveries about the balance of determinism and indeterminism in nature. 'If Jones had struck the window, it would have shattered' doesn't become false if scientists discover unexpected mixtures of determinism. This shows factualist analysis is wrong.

171. [EP-143] The Frequency Theory of Probability is false
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: The Frequency Theory of Probability (which identifies probabilities with observed frequencies) is false. Probabilities are determined by laws and causal mechanisms, not by statistical patterns. Identical laws yield identical probabilities regardless of statistical divergences, and different causal structures yield different probabilities regardless of statistical similarities.

172. [EP-143] The Frequency Theory of Probability is false
   Sources: WORK-014 (Chapter 7)
   Thesis: Frequency Theory (FT) - the view that probabilities are identical with statistical frequencies - is false. Refuted by W1/W2 thought experiment and smoking-cancer example.

173. [EP-144] Truth out of context as damaging as falsehood
   Sources: WORK-038 (Why Was Socrates Executed? Part I)
   Thesis: Truths taken out of context, or made available to people who don't know how to contextualize them, can be as damaging as falsehoods and lead to confusion that enables acceptance of falsehoods.

174. [EP-145] Plato's extreme rationalism is political, not epistemological
   Sources: WORK-038 (The Republic as Political Tract)
   Thesis: Plato's radical epistemological claim that observables are unreal 'shadows' while only pure thought accesses reality is not genuine epistemology but political belief presented as epistemological belief, driven by fear of political change.

175. [EP-146] Plato's epistemology rationalizes his social position
   Sources: WORK-038 (The Republic as Political Tract)
   Thesis: Plato's epistemological views make little sense as epistemology but make perfect sense as attempts to rationalize and preserve his privileged social position against threatening changes.

176. [EP-147] Descartes' epistemology responds to Church authority crisis
   Sources: WORK-038 (The Republic as Political Tract)
   Thesis: Descartes' epistemological project was fundamentally an attempt to cope with social unrest caused by science stripping Catholic Church of credibility. He sought new certainties to replace discredited Church certainties.

177. [EP-148] Extreme rationalism correlates with anxiety about social change
   Sources: WORK-038 (The Republic as Political Tract)
   Thesis: Both Plato and Descartes advocate extreme rationalism (overplaying role of thought, underplaying observation) because both were uneasy about social changes - Plato unambivalently hostile, Descartes ambivalently accepting.

178. [EP-149] Empiricizing philosophy enables world-engagement
   Sources: WORK-038 (Philosophy as Psychopathology)
   Thesis: Locke and Hume's empiricism represented attempt to make philosophy engage the world. Their political philosophies succeeded precisely to extent they empiricized/anti-philosophized philosophy.

179. [EP-150] Empiricism collapses into skepticism/autistic withdrawal
   Sources: WORK-038 (Philosophy as Psychopathology)
   Thesis: The empiricist project ultimately defeats itself: arch-empiricism (knowledge only of direct sensory evidence) collapses into pure skepticism (knowledge only of conscious states), representing the most complete autistic withdrawal from reality.

180. [ETH-008] Political Legitimacy Requires Both Effectiveness and Respect for Freedom
   Sources: Implicit in overall structure of argument and conclusion
   Thesis: A legitimate political system must satisfy both consequentialist criteria (it must actually work to improve people's lives) and deontological criteria (it must respect people's freedom and right to choose their own path). Systems that fail on either dimension are illegitimate.

181. [ETH-009] Pathological Concern with Ethics Derives from Crypto-Criminal Orientation
   Sources: Lines 35
   Thesis: Both obsessive-compulsives and philosophers are pathologically concerned with ethics because they are crypto-criminals whose fundamental orientation is anti-social, but who have draconian superegos and thus suppress their criminal side.

182. [ETH-010] Deontological Ethics Precludes Coercive Wealth Redistribution
   Sources: Chapter When is wealth-redistribution legitimate?
   Thesis: According to deontological ethics (Kant, Ross), wealth cannot legitimately be redistributed through coercion, even if equal distribution would be desirable. The means (coercing people, thwarting autonomy) cannot be justified by the ends (wealth equality).

183. [ETH-011] Kant and Ross Would Not Regard Wealth Equality as Particularly Desirable
   Sources: Chapter When is wealth-redistribution legitimate?
   Thesis: Even if there were morally innocuous way of achieving/maintaining wealth equality, neither Kant nor Ross would regard it as particularly desirable state of affairs. They would see it as desirable only to extent necessary for people to treat each other with respect - not clear that wealth equality particularly conducive to that end.

184. [ETH-012] Lies Cannot Be Justified by Unjust Ends They Serve
   Sources: Chapter Plato's Republic as Pol Potist Bureaucracy
   Thesis: If a conception of justice is fundamentally unjust (denying basic freedoms), then lies told to maintain that conception cannot be distinguished into good and bad lies. All lies serving unjust ends are thereby unjust regardless of intentions.

185. [ETH-013] Psychopathic Discourse as Career-Based Integrity Undermining
   Sources: Chapter Main text
   Thesis: When people engage in hand-waving about deconstructionism, narratives, and critiques, they are revealed as individuals who themselves have no integrity and whose careers constitute attempts to undermine the very notion of integrity. One should promptly disengage from such people.

186. [ETH-014] Psychopathy as Categorical Moral Incapacity
   Sources: Chapter Psychopathy definition
   Thesis: Psychopaths lack conscience and are fundamentally incapable of genuine moral engagement. Their callous disregard for others, manipulativeness, and deceitfulness constitute not mere moral failings but categorical incapacity for the moral domain. They cannot form the kinds of attachments and relationships that ground normal moral agency.

187. [ETHICS-001] Ability-Character Complementarity
   Sources: Multiple dialogues on ability, talent, and achievement
   Thesis: Human abilities differ little by nature; it is habits and character (diligence, persistence) that separate people. Genius is made through dilligence, not born.

188. [ETHICS-002] Process Over Outcomes in Evaluation
   Sources: Dialogues on failure, success, and perseverance
   Thesis: What matters is success relative to failure, not the absence of failure. 'Our greatest glory lies not in never falling but in getting up every time we do.' Successful people stumble many times; their successes, not their failures, define them.

189. [ETHICS-003] Pace Irrelevance in Progress
   Sources: Dialogue on speed vs. steadiness
   Thesis: The rate of progress doesn't matter; what matters is continuous forward motion. 'It does not matter how slowly you go, just as long as you do not stop.'

190. [ETHICS-004] Internal vs. External Worth
   Sources: Dialogue on fame and accomplishment
   Thesis: One should not worry that nobody knows who you are; worry that you are not worth knowing. The problem is not lack of recognition but lack of actual merit.

191. [ETHICS-005] Universal Teacherhood Principle
   Sources: Dialogue on learning from wise men vs. fools
   Thesis: Everyone can be a teacher: from superiors one learns what to do; from inferiors one learns what not to do. 'If I am with two men, one my inferior, the other my superior, each is my teacher.'

192. [ETHICS-006] Commitment Completeness Principle
   Sources: Dialogue on choosing between career paths
   Thesis: Whatever you do, do it with your whole heart. Full commitment to chosen path is more important than which path is chosen.

193. [ETHICS-007] Anger's Misguiding Influence
   Sources: Dialogues on anger and revenge
   Thesis: Under anger's influence, conduct is misguided. One should take a time-out and revisit situations from a calmer vantage. Before acting in anger, think of the consequences.

194. [FW-001] Conscious will as awareness of unconscious intention
   Sources: WORK-027 (lines 233-275)
   Thesis: The conscious event W that a subject experiences as their decision to act is not the operative intention itself, but rather an awareness of an intention that has already been formed unconsciously. The readiness potential (RP) precedes W by approximately 350ms.

195. [FW-002] W is not epiphenomenal despite lacking causal role
   Sources: WORK-027 (lines 252-266)
   Thesis: Although W (conscious awareness of intention) is epiphenomenal with respect to the particular finger-movement in Libet's experiment, it is not epiphenomenal tout court. W has causal effects on subsequent intentions and behaviors.

196. [FW-003] Libet challenges compatibilism more than determinism
   Sources: WORK-027 (lines 1617-1632)
   Thesis: The Libet experiment is more threatening to compatibilism than to traditional debates about determinism. The problem is not that choices are predetermined, but that what we experience as conscious decisions are 'on the sidelines'—not the operative causes at all.

197. [FW-004] Libet's results more radical than Freud's unconscious
   Sources: WORK-027 (lines 1648-1669)
   Thesis: Libet's findings are more radical than Freud's theory of the unconscious. Freud showed that some mental activity occurs unconsciously while treating consciousness as having real but limited power. Libet shows that even paradigmatic conscious decisions are not conscious decisions at all.

198. [FW-005] Consciousness integrates internal and external information
   Sources: WORK-027 (lines 1709-1728)
   Thesis: One purpose of consciousness is to integrate real-time awareness of external events (sense-perceptions) with real-time awareness of internal processes (introspections). Consciousness pools these two datasets, though decisions are made unconsciously.

199. [FW-006] Conscious awareness serves approval/veto function
   Sources: WORK-027 (lines 1728-1768)
   Thesis: W (conscious awareness) does not make decisions but rather approves or vetoes decisions already made unconsciously. Acts experienced as conscious decision-making are actually conscious acts of decision-approval. This approval extends to future similar intentions.

200. [FW-007] The illusion of free choice is functionally adaptive
   Sources: WORK-027 (lines 1750-1756)
   Thesis: The experience of conscious decision-making as the source of our actions (when it's actually approval of unconscious decisions) is an adaptive illusion that prevents consciousness from being overburdened. The 'illusion of free choice' is a small price to pay for cognitive efficiency.

201. [FW-008] Critique of 'phenomenological datum' argument for free will
   Sources: WORK-027 (lines 1547-1556)
   Thesis: The argument that we 'just know' we have free will because it's a phenomenological datum is question-begging and misconceived. The cogito is analytically true (thinking requires a thinker), but free will is a form of causation, and causal relations are always open empirical questions.

202. [FW-009] Critique of 'Libet actions don't engage rationality' objection
   Sources: WORK-027 (lines 1490-1502)
   Thesis: The objection that Libet's results don't generalize because they involve simple actions that fail to engage rationality is unsupported and likely false. First, there's no experimental data supporting this claim. Second, more deliberation makes it less likely that operative intentions are fully conscious.

203. [FW-010] Philosophers' rejection of Libet uses inadequate standards
   Sources: WORK-027 (lines 1813-1827)
   Thesis: Philosophers evaluating Libet's data employ standards of proof so stringent that by the same standards, there would be no reason to believe smoking causes cancer. By refusing to accept and model Libet's replicated findings, philosophers blind themselves to valuable information.

204. [FW-011] Standing intentions cannot be constituents of consciousness
   Sources: WORK-027 (lines 452-461)
   Thesis: Standing intentions (e.g., intention to write a book over three years) are enduring structures like knowledge that 2+2=4. They cannot be constituents of consciousness because consciousness involves momentary events. One can be conscious of standing intentions, but they are not themselves conscious states.

205. [FW-012] Libet's findings likely extend to complex deliberative actions
   Sources: WORK-027 (lines 433-448)
   Thesis: If intentions governing real-time actions (where consciousness is most heavily involved) are unconscious, it is doubly unlikely that intentions governing complex, deliberative actions are conscious. Complex intentions develop through protracted, largely unconscious thought-processes.

206. [FW-013] The 0.2 second delay is not mere tape-delay
   Sources: WORK-027 (lines 471-488)
   Thesis: The 0.2 second delay between intention (RP onset) and conscious awareness (W) is not merely the minimum processing time required for awareness. Consciousness operates extremely quickly—sprinters can be conscious of individual steps and sub-components. The delay likely reflects W's functional role as action-monitor rather than action-instigator.

207. [FW-014] Determinism defined as universal causation
   Sources: WORK-035
   Thesis: Determinism is the doctrine that nothing is uncaused. If determinism is correct, how the world is now determines exactly how it will be in the future, and how it was in the past determines exactly how it is now.

208. [FW-015] Indeterminism entails some chaos
   Sources: WORK-035
   Thesis: If indeterminism is true, the universe is chaotic to a non-null degree. A single uncaused event makes indeterminism correct, regardless of how minute that event is or how orderly the rest of the universe.

209. [FW-016] Indeterminism threatens freedom more than determinism
   Sources: WORK-035
   Thesis: Indeterminism is more of a threat to freedom than determinism. Determinism is not a threat to freedom but is actually necessary for it. Indeterminism is as fatal to freedom as determinism is necessary for it.

210. [FW-017] External indeterminism doesn't increase freedom
   Sources: WORK-035
   Thesis: If the external world (outside our bodies) were indeterministic while our minds/bodies remained deterministic, we would not be more free. In a highly indeterministic world where baseballs might turn into hawks, we would have less control and thus less freedom.

211. [FW-018] Internal indeterminism doesn't increase freedom
   Sources: WORK-035
   Thesis: If our nervous systems were indeterministic, we wouldn't be more free. An indeterministic nervous system means current state fails to determine later states, so decisions don't reliably lead to actions. Knowledge at one time wouldn't reliably influence decisions later.

212. [FW-019] Mechanisms require determinism
   Sources: WORK-035
   Thesis: Where there are mechanisms, there is determinism. Where there is indeterminism, there are no mechanisms. A mechanism is something that can be relied on to produce an output of a certain kind when given an input of a certain kind. Mechanisms exist only to the extent the universe is deterministic.

213. [FW-020] Indeterministic minds are incoherent
   Sources: WORK-035
   Thesis: The more indeterministic a mind is, the more incoherent it is - its future states fail to cohere with present states, present with past. An indeterministic mind is no mind at all, but rather a series of mutually isolated minds, each existing only for an instant and therefore not existing at all.

214. [FW-021] Freedom requires appropriate mental state responsiveness
   Sources: WORK-035
   Thesis: Inner freedom consists in one's current mental state embodying appropriate responsiveness to previous mental states, and future mental states embodying the right sort of responsiveness to present mental states. Freedom is about having a mind whose mental states determine one another in the right way, not about having undetermined mental states.

215. [FW-022] Freedom as causal integration
   Sources: WORK-035
   Thesis: A person is free if their mind is constitutive of the right sort of causal integration. Freedom requires both determinism (causal integrity) and the correct kind of causal structure, but determinism alone is not sufficient for freedom.

216. [FW-023] Responsibility requires agent-constitutive causation
   Sources: WORK-035
   Thesis: For an action to be free is for the agent to be responsible for it. For an agent to be responsible is for agent-constitutive forces to be responsible for it, which means for them to cause it. Prior to the agent's existence, we cannot speak of the agent being made to do anything. Once in existence, being made to do something consists in agent-constitutive forces being directed contrary to the agent's objectives; doing something freely consists in those forces aligning with the agent's objectives.

217. [HIST-001] Material Conditions Shape Consciousness
   Sources: WORK-030 (lines 13, 50-100)
   Thesis: Material developments (like writing and economic organization) fundamentally shape modes of consciousness, including temporal awareness and rationality.

218. [HIST-2] Socrates operationally trained oligarchic coup leaders
   Sources: WORK-038 (Why Was Socrates Executed? Part III)
   Thesis: Socrates didn't merely 'lay groundwork' for oligarchic coups - he operationally trained the men who tried to take over Athens (Critias, Alcibiades, Charmides). He was assembling a team, not innocently thinking.

219. [LANG-001] Semantics vs. Presemantics Distinction
   Sources: WORK-003
   Thesis: Literal meaning must be distinguished from information through which one identifies that meaning

220. [LANG-002] Universal Extensionality
   Sources: WORK-003
   Thesis: ALL contexts extensional at semantic level. Co-referring terms intersubstitutable.

221. [LANG-003] Direct Referentialism for Proper Names
   Sources: WORK-003
   Thesis: Proper names are mere labels referring directly to objects

222. [LANG-004] Direct Referentialism for Definite Descriptions
   Sources: WORK-003, WORK-004
   Thesis: Descriptions refer directly but communicate Russellian propositions

223. [LANG-005] Against Indirect Referentialism
   Sources: WORK-003
   Thesis: Frege's sense/reference theory for descriptions is incoherent

224. [LANG-006] Presemantic Implicature Theory
   Sources: WORK-003
   Thesis: New category beyond Gricean post-semantic implicature

225. [LANG-007] Definite Descriptions as Referring Expressions
   Sources: WORK-004
   Thesis: Definite descriptions ('the phi') are referring terms, not quantificational. Always encode propositions of form 'a has phi'

226. [LANG-008] Referential-Attributive Distinction as Pragmatic
   Sources: WORK-004
   Thesis: Not semantic ambiguity. Both uses involve reference. Attributive: reference to entity in understood existence claim. Referential: ostensive.

227. [LANG-009] Presupposition Not Assertion
   Sources: WORK-004
   Thesis: Following Strawson: descriptions presuppose existence. Abortive (not false) when presupposition fails.

228. [LANG-010] Against Contextualism
   Sources: WORK-004
   Thesis: Rejects neo-Russellian move that context restricts quantifier domains. Violates Gricean principles.

229. [LANG-011] Against Conventional Implicature Solution
   Sources: WORK-004
   Thesis: D-sentences don't implicate object-dependent propositions via conventional implicature. Violates implicature principles.

230. [LANG-012] Anaphoric Descriptions as Referential
   Sources: WORK-004
   Thesis: Anaphoric descriptions refer to entities introduced by antecedents. Not bound variables.

231. [LANG-013] Logical Form vs Semantics
   Sources: WORK-004
   Thesis: Logical form (inferential structure) ≠ semantics (literal meaning). Same proposition can have multiple logical forms.

232. [LANG-014] Logical Form vs Grammatical Form (Frege's Insight)
   Sources: WORK-001 (Chapter 1)
   Thesis: Logical form is not identical with grammatical form. What a sentence says is not always what its grammatical structure suggests.

233. [LANG-015] Propositions as Abstract Objects
   Sources: WORK-001 (Chapter 3)
   Thesis: Propositions are abstract, mind-independent objects that exist outside space and time.

234. [LANG-016] Truth as Correspondence
   Sources: WORK-001 (Chapter 3)
   Thesis: A proposition is true iff it corresponds to reality—if the world is as it says the world is.

235. [LANG-017] Meaning is Not Mental
   Sources: WORK-001 (Chapter 4)
   Thesis: Linguistic meaning is not reducible to mental states or psychological facts.

236. [LANG-018] Russell's Theory of Descriptions
   Sources: WORK-001 (Chapter 6)
   Thesis: Definite descriptions are not referring terms. 'The F is G' means 'Something uniquely is F, and any such thing is G.'

237. [LANG-019] Frege's Sense/Reference Distinction Collapses into Russellianism
   Sources: WORK-001 (Chapter 6)
   Thesis: Frege's theory that definite descriptions have both sense and reference collapses into Russell's theory.

238. [LANG-020] Against Fregean Intensional Contexts
   Sources: WORK-001 (Chapter 6)
   Thesis: Frege's distinction between extensional and intensional contexts is a failed epicycle.

239. [LANG-021] Literal Meaning vs Cognitive Significance
   Sources: WORK-001 (Chapter 8)
   Thesis: Literal meaning (semantic content) must be distinguished from cognitive significance.

240. [LANG-022] Semantic Externalism (Putnam's Insight)
   Sources: WORK-001 (Chapter 9)
   Thesis: Meanings of natural kind terms are partially determined by facts external to the speaker's mind.

241. [LANG-023] Systematicity and Productivity of Language
   Sources: WORK-006
   Thesis: Languages exhibit two fundamental properties: (1) Productivity - expressions can be combined into larger expressions, and (2) Systematicity - parts composing complex expressions can be re-arranged to form other expressions.

242. [LANG-024] Semantic Rules Are Ultimately Descriptive
   Sources: WORK-007 Paper 1
   Thesis: Semantic rules describe what speakers mean/understand by expressions. They are not purely conventional stipulations independent of speaker practices. For 'red' to mean red just IS for speakers to mean/understand red by 'red'.

243. [LANG-025] Names as Concept-Laden Labels
   Sources: Solution to Kripke's Paradox
   Thesis: Names are mere labels (non-descriptive), as Kripke argued, but labels 'pick up' descriptive information through how one learns their reference. Semantics (what expressions literally mean) differs from knowledge of semantics (how meaning is known), and this difference explains apparent descriptive content.

244. [LANG-026] Co-referential Names Can Differ in Meaning
   Sources: Solution to Frege's Puzzle
   Thesis: 'Superman' and 'Clark Kent' differ in meaning despite co-referring. 'Superman is in the building' says that a certain property-set (superhuman strength, flight ability, etc.) is uniquely instantiated and any such instance is in the building. 'Clark Kent is in the building' says a different property-set (reporter, wears glasses, etc.) is uniquely instantiated.

245. [LANG-027] Semantics-Pragmatics Distinction is Real
   Sources: Essay 6
   Thesis: LLMs' demonstrated capabilities provide empirical support for classical theories of meaning, particularly the distinction between semantics (literal meaning) and pragmatics (communicated meaning), and the reality of compositional literal meaning.

246. [LANG-028] Compositional Understanding Emerges from Statistical Learning
   Sources: Essay 6
   Thesis: Compositional capabilities can emerge through statistical learning rather than requiring classical computational implementation. Both humans and AI develop compositional understanding through different but convergent paths.

247. [LANG-029] Literal Meaning is Real but Emergent
   Sources: Essay 6
   Thesis: Literal meaning is real rather than illusory, but emergent rather than primary. It develops through triangulation from contextual uses rather than being foundational to language.

248. [LANG-030] Grammar and Logic Are Aligned
   Sources: Essay 7
   Thesis: LLMs' ability to make correct inferences without FOL-style logical forms suggests grammatical structure itself guides valid reasoning. Perceived misalignment between grammatical and logical form may be artifact of our chosen formal systems rather than feature of language itself.

249. [LANG-031] Logical Form is Cognitively Inert
   Sources: Essay 7
   Thesis: Traditional logical form is cognitively inert: people reason without it, LLMs function without it, it plays no role in actual inference. Logical form is constructed rather than discovered, follows rather than guides comprehension, and is system-relative rather than unique.

250. [LANG-032] Universal Grammar as Neural Architecture
   Sources: Essay 9
   Thesis: Chomskyan insights about linguistic universals can be reconciled with connectionism by understanding UG as feature of neural architecture rather than set of explicit rules. UG constraints operate through architectural properties of neural networks rather than explicit rule-following.

251. [LANG-033] AI Vindicates Semantics-Pragmatics Distinction
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 6: AI Vindicates Classical Theories of Meaning)
   Thesis: Large language models demonstrate a clear distinction between semantic content (literal meaning) and pragmatic interpretation (context-dependent meaning). The success of LLMs requires maintaining this distinction, vindicating classical theories of meaning against pragmatist attempts to collapse semantics into pragmatics.

252. [LANG-034] Literal Meaning is Computationally Prior to Pragmatic Interpretation
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 6)
   Thesis: AI systems must compute literal semantic content before applying pragmatic adjustments. This computational priority reveals that semantics is foundational and pragmatics is derivative, supporting classical over post-modern theories of meaning.

253. [LANG-035] Context-Independence of Core Semantics
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 6)
   Thesis: LLMs demonstrate that core semantic content can be represented context-independently and then applied across contexts. This vindicates the classical view that meaning has a stable core that pragmatic factors modulate rather than wholly constitute.

254. [LANG-036] AI Vindicates Syntax-Semantics Interface
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 7: AI Vindicates Classical Grammar)
   Thesis: Large language models demonstrate that grammatical structure (syntax) and meaning (semantics) are distinct but systematically related. The success of LLMs requires explicit representation of this interface, vindicating classical grammatical theory against attempts to reduce one to the other.

255. [LANG-037] Syntactic Structure Constrains Semantic Interpretation
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 7)
   Thesis: AI language systems reveal that syntactic structure constrains possible semantic interpretations. This supports the classical view that grammar is not arbitrary convention but reflects systematic constraints on meaning composition.

256. [LANG-038] Compositional Semantics Requires Syntactic Structure
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 7)
   Thesis: The ability of LLMs to understand novel sentences demonstrates that semantic interpretation is compositional - the meaning of complex expressions derives systematically from their parts and syntactic structure. This requires and vindicates traditional compositional semantic theories.

257. [LANG-039] Grammar and Logic are Fundamentally Aligned
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 8: Grammar-Logic Alignment)
   Thesis: AI language systems demonstrate that grammatical structure and logical form are fundamentally aligned - grammatical categories and relations correspond to logical types and operations. This alignment is not accidental but reflects deep unity of form in human cognition.

258. [LANG-040] Universal Grammar Can Be Implemented Subsymbolically
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 12: Chomskyan Challenge)
   Thesis: AI demonstrates that universal grammar principles can be implemented through subsymbolic connectionist architectures rather than requiring explicit symbolic rules. The functional role of UG is preserved even if implementation differs from classical theory.

259. [LANG-041] Universal Grammar Principles Extend to Music
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 15: UG in Language and Music)
   Thesis: AI analysis reveals that the computational principles underlying universal grammar in language also apply to music cognition. Both domains share abstract structural principles about hierarchical organization and rule-governed combination.

260. [LANG-045] Presupposition Versus Assertion in Perceptual Reports
   Sources: Chapter Objection and reply
   Thesis: When sensations are property-indeterminate (as in the tie shop), one cannot assert 'the tie is maroon' based on sensation alone. One might respond that the sensation presupposes rather than asserts a determinacy. But this conflates epistemology with semantics - the point is that sensation doesn't tell you which property obtains, not that it pragmatically presupposes one.

261. [LANG-046] 'Some' Presupposes Rather Than Asserts Existence
   Sources: Chapter Point #1 and #2
   Thesis: 'Some phi is a psi' is not an existence-claim. It does not assert that there exists at least one phi that is also a psi. Rather, it merely presupposes that there is at least one phi. This concerns literal meaning (semantics), not what is conveyed by implicature.

262. [LANG-047] 'All' is Incompatible With 'Some'
   Sources: Chapter Point #3
   Thesis: 'All phi's are psi's' is incompatible with 'some phi's are psi's.' The former entails the negation of the latter and thus isn't compatible with it. This concerns literal meaning, not pragmatic implicature.

263. [LANG-048] Numerals Express Exact Quantities
   Sources: Chapter Point #4
   Thesis: For any numeral n, 'n phi's are psi's' is to the effect that exactly n phi's are psi's. 'Five elephants can dance' means exactly five elephants can dance, not 'at least five.' If six elephants can dance, the statement 'five elephants can dance' is literally false, not merely misleading.

264. [LANG-049] Incompatibility of Different Numeral Statements
   Sources: Chapter Point #5
   Thesis: If m≠n, 'm phi's are psi's' is incompatible with 'n phi's are psi's.' 'Ten elephants can dance' is incompatible with 'five elephants can dance.' This is an immediate consequence of numerals expressing exact quantities.

265. [LANG-050] Numerals as Substantives Versus Adjectives
   Sources: Chapter Substantive vs. adjective argument
   Thesis: In '5 + 5 = 10,' numerals function as substantives (nouns), synonymous with 'the number 5' and 'the number 10.' In '5 things plus 5 other things add up to exactly 10 things,' the same numerals function as adjectives. Despite this syntactic difference, the expressions have extremely similar meanings, which would be impossible if numerals meant 'at least n.'

266. [LANG-051] Sentence Truth-Conditions Versus Proposition Truth-Conditions
   Sources: Chapter Preliminary distinctions
   Thesis: The logical properties of propositions aren't always in lockstep with those of the sentences (or sentence-tokens) that express them. For definite descriptions with Strawson-type semantics, the sentence 'that phi is psi' has different truth-conditions from the proposition it expresses in context.

267. [LANG-052] Response Adequacy Test for Existence Claims
   Sources: Chapter Similar argument
   Thesis: If asked 'do any felines exist?', responding '(A) Yes. There exists at least one feline; moreover, at least one feline is ferocious' is completely non-defective, but responding '(B) some felines are ferocious' is defective. This shows (B) presupposes rather than asserts existence, distinguishing it from (A).

268. [LANG-053] Anti-Gricean Analysis of Quantifiers and Numerals
   Sources: Chapter Throughout - explicit rejection of implicature analysis
   Thesis: The correct analysis of 'some,' 'all,' and numerals concerns literal meaning (semantics), not conversational implicature. 'Some' means 'some but not all' at the level of semantics, not just by Gricean implicature. Similarly, 'five' means 'exactly five' semantically, not 'at least five' with an implicature of exactness.

269. [LANG-055] There are different ways of picking out a number: canonical (using usual numerical notation) vs. non-canonical descriptions
   Sources: WORK-008 (Berry's Paradox)
   Thesis: There are different ways of picking out a number: canonical (using usual numerical notation) vs. non-canonical descriptions

270. [LANG-056] Taken in isolation, a given action can be interpreted as compliance with any of a plurality of incompatible rules, but independent evidence disambiguates
   Sources: WORK-008 (Wittgenstein's Rule-following Paradox)
   Thesis: Taken in isolation, a given action can be interpreted as compliance with any of a plurality of incompatible rules, but independent evidence disambiguates

271. [LANG-057] Wittgenstein's 'paradox' collapses into the trivial point that erroneous hypotheses may be compatible with artificially restricted data sets
   Sources: WORK-008 (Wittgenstein's Rule-following Paradox)
   Thesis: Wittgenstein's 'paradox' collapses into the trivial point that erroneous hypotheses may be compatible with artificially restricted data sets

272. [LANG-058] Quine's 'indeterminacy of translation' thesis only appears true if one considers artificially constricted data-sets
   Sources: WORK-008 (Wittgenstein's Rule-following Paradox)
   Thesis: Quine's 'indeterminacy of translation' thesis only appears true if one considers artificially constricted data-sets

273. [LANG-059] Sorites paradoxes are solved by replacing binary characterizations with comparatives plus contextually supplied benchmarks
   Sources: WORK-008 (Sorites Paradox)
   Thesis: Sorites paradoxes are solved by replacing binary characterizations with comparatives plus contextually supplied benchmarks

274. [LANG-060] Vague terms like 'heap', 'rich', 'part of cloud' implicitly invoke contextual benchmarks; n+1 items are always 'more' than n items relative to some standard
   Sources: WORK-008 (Sorites Paradox)
   Thesis: Vague terms like 'heap', 'rich', 'part of cloud' implicitly invoke contextual benchmarks; n+1 items are always 'more' than n items relative to some standard

275. [LANG-061] 'Superman is in the building' says that a certain property-set (being superhumanly strong, able to fly, etc.) is uniquely instantiated and in the building
   Sources: WORK-008 (Frege's Puzzle)
   Thesis: 'Superman is in the building' says that a certain property-set (being superhumanly strong, able to fly, etc.) is uniquely instantiated and in the building

276. [LANG-062] Names are non-descriptive mere labels (Kripke is right about this)
   Sources: WORK-008 (Kripke's Paradox)
   Thesis: Names are non-descriptive mere labels (Kripke is right about this)

277. [LANG-063] Even mere labels 'pick up' descriptive information; one doesn't learn what a name refers to in a vacuum
   Sources: WORK-008 (Kripke's Paradox)
   Thesis: Even mere labels 'pick up' descriptive information; one doesn't learn what a name refers to in a vacuum

278. [LANG-064] There is semantics (what expressions literally mean) and there is knowledge of semantics; how one knows literal meaning may affect what sentences mean to that person
   Sources: WORK-008 (Kripke's Paradox)
   Thesis: There is semantics (what expressions literally mean) and there is knowledge of semantics; how one knows literal meaning may affect what sentences mean to that person

279. [LANG-065] Two utterances with the same literal meaning may convey very different bodies of information to those who know that literal meaning
   Sources: WORK-008 (Kripke's Paradox)
   Thesis: Two utterances with the same literal meaning may convey very different bodies of information to those who know that literal meaning

280. [LANG-28] No entity has both meaning and reference
   Sources: WORK-033 (lines 115-116)
   Thesis: Reference is a property of spatio-temporal entities (expression tokens). Sense is a property of Platonic entities (expression types). No single entity possesses both properties. The Fregean picture of expressions having both sense and reference is confused.

281. [LANG-29] All meaningful expression-tokens refer
   Sources: WORK-033 (lines 117)
   Thesis: On the correct conception of reference, all meaningful expression-tokens refer, including syncategorematic expressions like 'or', 'if', and case-markers. These refer to higher-order functions. The categorematic/syncategorematic distinction collapses given a proper understanding of reference.

282. [LANG-30] Sentence-tokens refer to propositions (or proposition-involving functions)
   Sources: WORK-033 (lines 137, 174-178)
   Thesis: Sentence-tokens refer. Forceless sentence-tokens (those embedded in larger sentences without force) refer to propositions. Forced sentence-tokens (those with assertoric or other force) refer to functions involving propositions, not identical with propositions.

283. [LANG-31] Sentence-types have functions for meanings, not propositions
   Sources: WORK-033 (lines 186-197)
   Thesis: Sentence-types do not have propositions for their meanings. All sentence-types uniformly have functions from contexts of utterance to propositions for their meanings. This holds even for seemingly non-indexical sentences like '2+2=4'—the uniformity of the class 'sentence' requires this.

284. [LANG-32] Expression-types do not refer; only tokens refer
   Sources: WORK-033 (lines 185, 199)
   Thesis: Expression-types categorically cannot refer to anything. Even the expression-type 'Plato' doesn't refer to Plato; it has a constant function (from contexts to Plato) for its meaning. Only tokens of 'Plato' refer to Plato.

285. [LANG-33] Replacing co-referring terms cannot change meaning
   Sources: WORK-033 (lines 946-990)
   Thesis: If two terms genuinely co-refer (refer to the same thing), then replacing one with the other cannot result in a change of meaning. What it means to say that terms co-refer just is that sentences differing only in those terms have the same meaning. All contexts are extensional at the level of literal meaning.

286. [LANG-34] Sentences do not refer to truth-values
   Sources: WORK-033 (lines 127, 134-137, 160)
   Thesis: Frege's thesis that sentences refer to truth-values is false. Sentence-tokens do refer, but not to truth-values. Forceless sentence-tokens refer to propositions; forced sentence-tokens refer to functions involving propositions.

287. [LANG-35] Apparent failures of extensionality explained by literal vs. communicated meaning
   Sources: WORK-033 (lines Chapter 20 title, Chapter 18 content)
   Thesis: Apparent counterexamples to extensionality (like belief contexts and modal contexts) result from confusing literal meaning with communicated meaning. 'John believes the inventor of bifocals snored' and 'John believes the first postmaster general snored' differ in what they communicate but coincide in literal meaning.

288. [LANG-36] Frege's sense-reference distinction is confused
   Sources: WORK-033 (lines Chapter 9, 115-116)
   Thesis: The Fregean sense-reference distinction involves fundamental confusions. Sense should not be conceived as a mode of presentation possessed by referring expressions. Rather, sense is a property of types (functions from contexts to referents) and reference is a property of tokens.

289. [LOGIC-001] Analytic Truth Defined by Content Alone
   Sources: WORK-005 (Essay 4)
   Thesis: Analytic truths hold entirely in virtue of own content. Given only what they say, could not fail to be true.

290. [LOGIC-002] Tautologies are NOT Analytic Truths
   Sources: WORK-005 (Essay 4)
   Thesis: Tautologies = definitional truths about word usage (empirical). No analytic truths are tautologies.

291. [LOGIC-003] All Mathematical Truths are Analytic
   Sources: WORK-005 (Essay 4)
   Thesis: Every mathematical truth is analytic. Mathematical knowledge = non-empirical knowledge of conceptual relations.

292. [LOGIC-004] Not All Analytic Truths are Mathematical
   Sources: WORK-005 (Essay 4)
   Thesis: Philosophy has its own domain of analytic truths. Example: 'All math truths are analytic' is analytic but philosophical.

293. [LOGIC-005] Analytic Truth vs. Formal Truth Distinction
   Sources: WORK-005 (Essay 4)
   Thesis: Analytic truth ≠ formal truth. Formally true = recursively definable. Math truths analytic but not formal.

294. [LOGIC-006] Incompleteness as Non-Recursive-Definability
   Sources: WORK-005 (Essay 4)
   Thesis: Incomplete = no recursive definition. Most scientifically important statement-sets incomplete.

295. [LOGIC-007] Sorites Vagueness Admitted
   Sources: Discussion of Sorites Paradox
   Thesis: The sorites paradox is genuine and shows that vague predicates lack sharp boundaries. There are borderline cases where no determinate fact makes a predicate apply or not apply.

296. [LOGIC-008] Logic is Incomplete (More General than Gödel)
   Sources: WORK-005 (Essay 4)
   Thesis: Class of all logics not itself recursively definable. Logic is incomplete. More general than Gödel's result.

297. [LOGIC-009] Logic as Recursively Defined Statement-Set
   Sources: WORK-005 (Essay 4)
   Thesis: A logic = recursively defined statement-set = axiomatic system

298. [LOGIC-010] Logicism Defended (Frege's Project)
   Sources: WORK-001 (Chapter 7)
   Thesis: Arithmetic is reducible to logic. Mathematical truths are logical truths.

299. [LOGIC-011] Classical Logic as Formalization Not Discovery
   Sources: Essay 1
   Thesis: Classical logic is fundamentally inadequate as a tool for reasoning because it requires more intelligence to recognize that an inference instantiates a logical law than to recognize the validity of the inference directly. Logic organizes existing knowledge about entailment but cannot generate new knowledge about entailment relationships.

300. [LOGIC-012] Performance vs. Competence-Demanding Inferences
   Sources: Essay 1
   Thesis: Two fundamentally different types of inferential challenges exist: (1) Performance-demanding (straining computational/memory resources), (2) Competence-demanding (requiring genuine insight). Classical logic can only assist with the former.

301. [LOGIC-013] System L: AI-Based Discovery Logic
   Sources: Essay 1
   Thesis: A radically different logical system exists in AI that actually helps reasoning through: (1) semantic networks representing concepts as relationship nodes, (2) meta-reasoning patterns generating new inferences, (3) defeasible reasoning allowing provisional inferences.

302. [LOGIC-014] Discovery-Justification Distinction in Logic
   Sources: Essay 1
   Thesis: System L maintains crucial distinction between discovery procedures (can use any methods including inductive/analogical) and verification procedures (must employ deductive methods in deductive domains). Avoids psychologism while enabling reasoning assistance.

303. [LOGIC-015] AI Logic as Ampliative vs. Classical Logic as Transformative
   Sources: Essay 1
   Thesis: AI-logic is inherently ampliative (generating genuinely new knowledge), while classical logic is purely transformative (rearranging existing knowledge). Only AI-logic can serve as genuine discovery tool.

304. [LOGIC-016] Classical Logic Fails as a Tool for Reasoning
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 1: AI Logic vs. Classical Logic - Part 1)
   Thesis: Classical logic fundamentally fails as a tool for reasoning because recognizing that an inference instantiates a logical law requires more intelligence than recognizing the validity of the inference directly. Logic organizes existing knowledge about entailment but cannot generate new knowledge.

305. [LOGIC-017] Performance-Demanding vs Competence-Demanding Inferences Distinction
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 1 - Part 1, distinction section)
   Thesis: Inferential challenges divide into performance-demanding (requiring computational/memory resources) and competence-demanding (requiring genuine insight). Classical logic can only assist with the former, not the latter - the very kind that most need logical assistance.

306. [LOGIC-018] Gödel's Incompleteness Refutes Logic as Mathematical Foundation
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 1 - Part 1, response to logicist project)
   Thesis: Gödel's demonstration that not even arithmetic is recursively definable makes the reduction of mathematics to logic impossible in principle. Logic cannot both justify and generate mathematical knowledge as was hoped.

307. [LOGIC-019] System L: Discovery Logic via AI
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 1 - Part 2: System L introduction)
   Thesis: A new logical system (System L) embodied in certain AI forms represents a decisive break from classical logic. It employs semantic networks, meta-reasoning patterns, and defeasible reasoning to actually help with competence-demanding inferences rather than merely cataloging known-valid inferences.

308. [LOGIC-020] Semantic Networks Enable Competence-Level Reasoning
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 1 - Part 2, first mechanism of System L)
   Thesis: System L represents concepts as nodes in a vast web of relationships capturing not just formal logical connections but probabilistic associations, causal links, and analogical mappings. This enables insight-level reasoning that formal logic cannot provide.

309. [LOGIC-021] Meta-Reasoning Patterns Generate New Inferences
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 1 - Part 2, second mechanism)
   Thesis: System L uses higher-order templates for generating new inferences that go beyond simple deductive rules. Example template: 'If capability X requires mechanism Y, and mechanism Y can malfunction in way Z, then X implies the potential for Z-type malfunctions.'

310. [LOGIC-022] Defeasible Reasoning Mirrors Actual Human Reasoning
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 1 - Part 2, third mechanism)
   Thesis: Incorporating defeasible reasoning - provisional inferences that can be revised in light of new information - better mirrors actual human reasoning and provides more practical utility than classical logic's requirement for absolute certainty.

311. [LOGIC-023] Grammatical Structure Guides Logical Inference
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 8: Grammar-Logic Alignment)
   Thesis: LLMs successfully perform logical reasoning by following grammatical structure, demonstrating that surface grammar encodes logical relationships. This vindicates the traditional view that grammatical form reveals logical form.

312. [LOGIC-024] Entailment Defined as Logical Consequence
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: P entails Q if and only if Q is a logical consequence of P. If P entails Q and P is true, then Q must be true and may be legitimately inferred from P.

313. [LOGIC-025] Confirmation Defined as Probability-Increase
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: P confirms Q if and only if P, supposing it true, increases the likelihood that Q is true. Confirmation does not by itself license inference from P to Q.

314. [LOGIC-026] Conditions for Legitimate Inductive Inference
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: Q may legitimately be inferred from P if and only if: (a) the supposition that P is true and Q is false has fewer unexplainable consequences than the supposition that P and Q are both true, AND (b) the supposition that P is true has fewer unexplainable consequences than the supposition that P is false.

315. [LOGIC-027] Four Principles: Entailment vs. Confirmation
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: Four logical principles hold: (i) Entailment is transitive; (ii) Confirmation is not transitive; (iii) Entailment is monotonic; (iv) Confirmation is non-monotonic. These differences show entailment and confirmation are fundamentally different relations.

316. [LOGIC-028] Entailment Not Maximal Confirmation
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: Entailment is not maximal confirmation. Entailment is a logical notion concerning what is counterlogical; confirmation is an explanatory notion concerning what is counterexplanatory.

317. [LOGIC-029] Confirmation-Relations Non-Objectual in Nature
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: Confirmation relations, though not themselves causal relations, sometimes hold in virtue of causal relations. But confirmation relates propositions, not objects or events.

318. [LOGIC-030] The Counterfactual Analysis of Causality (CAC) Is False
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: The Counterfactual Analysis of Causality - the view that 'e caused e*' means 'if e hadn't happened, e* wouldn't have happened' - is both extensionally and intensionally false.

319. [LOGIC-036] Generalization from Color to All Perceivable Properties
   Sources: Chapter Generalization argument
   Thesis: The argument about color-indeterminacy of sensations under non-standard lighting conditions generalizes to any perceivable property P. Distance perception provides the key example: when viewing through variable magnification (microscope/telescope goggles), visual sensations are distance- and size-indeterminate until background knowledge establishes the correspondence rule.

320. [LOGIC-037] Standard Logical Quantifier Semantics Conflicts With Natural Language
   Sources: Chapter Introduction and throughout
   Thesis: Most contemporary logicians and philosophers assume or explicitly state that each of the five points (about 'some,' 'all,' and numerals) is false, and much of what they say crumbles unless they're right. But the points are correct, showing standard logical semantics for quantifiers and numerals diverges from natural language meanings.

321. [LOGIC-038] Validity of Argument Reveals Semantic Content
   Sources: Chapter Validity argument
   Thesis: The argument 'Given that Smith has seven cars, he has an odd number of cars' is valid. This reveals that 'seven' means exactly seven, not 'at least seven,' since if 'seven' meant 'at least seven,' the argument would be invalid (as 'at least seven' is compatible with even numbers).

322. [LOGIC-039] 'True of itself' when meaningful abbreviates some non-reflexive construction; when it doesn't, 'self' is an undefined pronoun (free variable)
   Sources: WORK-008 (Heterological Paradox)
   Thesis: 'True of itself' when meaningful abbreviates some non-reflexive construction; when it doesn't, 'self' is an undefined pronoun (free variable)

323. [LOGIC-040] It is propositions (underlying meanings), not words per se, that are true or false
   Sources: WORK-008 (Liar Paradox)
   Thesis: It is propositions (underlying meanings), not words per se, that are true or false

324. [LOGIC-041] If h is synonymous with 'the referent of h+1', then h is defined in terms of an expression itself defined in terms of h, and h consequently has no referent
   Sources: WORK-008 (Hilbert-Bernays Paradox)
   Thesis: If h is synonymous with 'the referent of h+1', then h is defined in terms of an expression itself defined in terms of h, and h consequently has no referent

325. [LOGIC-042] There is no recursive definition of the class of recursive definitions (Kuczynski's Recursion Paradox)
   Sources: WORK-008 (Kuczynski's Recursion Paradox)
   Thesis: There is no recursive definition of the class of recursive definitions (Kuczynski's Recursion Paradox)

326. [LOGIC-043] There is no formal characterization of formal truth (corollary of Recursion Paradox)
   Sources: WORK-008 (Kuczynski's Recursion Paradox - Corollary)
   Thesis: There is no formal characterization of formal truth (corollary of Recursion Paradox)

327. [LOGIC-044] Everything can be automated except for automation itself (Automation Paradox)
   Sources: WORK-008 (Automation Paradox)
   Thesis: Everything can be automated except for automation itself (Automation Paradox)

328. [LOGIC-045] Trinity doctrine is logically incoherent
   Sources: WORK-028 (lines 528-540)
   Thesis: The orthodox Christian doctrine of the Trinity ('three persons, one God') is logically incoherent. If God, Jesus, and Holy Spirit are genuinely three distinct persons, there are three gods (polytheism). If they're not genuinely distinct, the Trinity collapses into modalism or unitarianism. No coherent interpretation makes them both three and one.

329. [LOGIC-046] Tractatus vs. Later Wittgenstein on Contradiction
   Sources: WORK-031 (lines 320-327)
   Thesis: Wittgenstein's early view (Tractatus) that all inconsistency reduces to explicit contradiction was questioned in his later work, which recognized irreducible forms of inconsistency.

330. [LOGIC-047] Set-Theoretic Foundations and Paradoxes
   Sources: WORK-031 (lines 730-793, 794-830)
   Thesis: Attempts to use set theory as a foundation for semantics and modal metaphysics face fundamental paradoxes related to self-reference and circularity.

331. [LOGIC-052] Logic as the study of formal truth
   Sources: WORK-033
   Thesis: Logic is the study of formal truth. A formal truth is a statement that holds entirely in virtue of its own structural properties, such as 'it is either raining or it isn't' or 'there are continuous functions that cannot be differentiated at any point.'

332. [LOGIC-053] Mathematical logic studies statement-functions
   Sources: WORK-033
   Thesis: Mathematical logic is logic done with mathematical concepts and techniques. The central concept of mathematics is function (a rule assigning objects to objects). Mathematical logic studies statement-functions - functions that assign statements to statements - while other branches study numerical functions.

333. [LOGIC-054] Negation preserves incompatibility
   Sources: WORK-033
   Thesis: The negation of a statement is incompatible with that statement - they cannot simultaneously be true. To properly negate a statement, put 'it is not the case that' in front of it. The negation of 'someone is tall' is 'nobody is tall' (not 'someone is not tall'), since the latter can be true simultaneously with the former.

334. [LOGIC-055] Truth-functional operators defined by truth tables
   Sources: WORK-033
   Thesis: Conjunction, disjunction, and negation are truth-functional: whether the output sentence is true depends only on whether input sentences are true. 'P and Q' is true iff both P and Q are true. 'P or Q' is false iff both P and Q are false. 'Not-P' is true iff P is false.

335. [LOGIC-056] Non-truth-functional operators exist
   Sources: WORK-033
   Thesis: 'Because' and epistemic operators like 'Jim knows that' are non-truth-functional. Even if P and Q are both true, 'P because Q' isn't necessarily true. Similarly, given only that P is true, it doesn't follow that 'Jim knows that P' is true.

336. [LOGIC-057] Recursive functions define logical systems
   Sources: WORK-033
   Thesis: Each logical function (negation, conjunction, disjunction) is defined for its own outputs - you can negate negations, conjoin conjunctions, etc., indefinitely. This means each function is recursive. A system of logic is a recursively defined statement class, and logic is the study of such classes. This is what it means to say logic is the study of formal truth.

337. [LOGIC-48] The Slingshot argument is unsound
   Sources: WORK-033 (lines 158-162, 974-999)
   Thesis: The Slingshot argument, which purports to show that sentences refer to truth-values, contains profound errors and proves things that are obviously false (e.g., that your car is identical with Mozart's favorite wig). The argument must be rejected by finding its errors rather than accepting its conclusions.

338. [META-001] Platonism about Properties
   Sources: WORK-001 (Chapter 2)
   Thesis: Properties exist as non-spatiotemporal entities distinct from instances

339. [META-002] Propositions as Sets of Properties
   Sources: WORK-001 (Chapter 3)
   Thesis: Propositions are sets of properties; truth is instantiatedness

340. [META-003] Anti-Constructivism
   Sources: WORK-002 (BEAUTYBLISS07 exchange)
   Thesis: Constructivism self-refuting through reductio

341. [META-004] Properties as Non-Spatiotemporal Entities (Platonism)
   Sources: WORK-001 (Chapter 2)
   Thesis: Properties exist as non-spatiotemporal entities. They are not identical with their instances.

342. [META-005] Uninstantiated Properties Exist
   Sources: WORK-001 (Chapter 2)
   Thesis: Properties can exist even when nothing has them. The property of being a unicorn exists.

343. [META-006] Determinism vs Randomness vs Unpredictability
   Sources: WORK-001 (Chapter 14)
   Thesis: Determinism, randomness, and predictability are distinct concepts that must not be conflated.

344. [META-007] Personal Identity Through Time
   Sources: WORK-001 (Chapter 16)
   Thesis: Personal identity consists in psychological continuity, not bodily continuity.

345. [META-008] Causality as Counterfactual Dependence
   Sources: WORK-001 (Chapter 17)
   Thesis: Event A causes event B iff: (1) both occur, and (2) had A not occurred, B would not have occurred.

346. [META-011] Binary Truth Despite Continuous Representation
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 20: Binary Truth)
   Thesis: AI systems demonstrate that truth remains fundamentally binary (true/false) even though cognitive systems represent information continuously. Continuous confidence values or fuzzy representations are epistemic features, not ontological features of truth itself.

347. [META-012] Causation as the Essence of Temporal Precedence
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: For one event e to precede another event e* is for there to be a possible causal process beginning with e and ending with e*. Simultaneity is the absence of such a process.

348. [META-013] Continuity as the Essence of Causation
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: Effects are continuations of their causes. Causation essentially involves continuity - causal processes preserve structural relationships across temporal intervals without gaps.

349. [META-014] Causation a Relation Between Events, Not Objects
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: Causation is fundamentally a relation between events, not between objects. Objects participate in causal relations only derivatively, in virtue of events involving those objects.

350. [META-015] Conditions Identical with Event-Patterns
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: Conditions are identical with event-patterns. A condition is not a state but rather a pattern or type of event sequence that can be instantiated multiple times.

351. [META-016] So-Called Changes Typically Identical with Meta-Changes
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: What are ordinarily called changes are typically meta-changes - changes in the rate, direction, or pattern of already-occurring changes rather than absolute transitions from rest to motion or vice versa.

352. [META-017] Threshold Effects and Psychological Overdetermination
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: In psychology, causation often involves exceeding thresholds rather than simple sufficient conditions. An effect may require the confluence of multiple reasons, none sufficient alone but jointly sufficient once a threshold is exceeded.

353. [META-018] Threshold Effects and the Analog-Digital Distinction
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: Discontinuous changes tend to involve threshold effects; continuous changes do not. To the extent the world changes discontinuously it has digital structure; to the extent continuously, analog structure.

354. [META-019] Causes as INUS-Conditions
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: In most contexts, to say 'X caused Y' means 'X was an INUS condition for Y' - an Insufficient but Non-redundant part of an Unnecessary but Sufficient condition for Y's occurrence.

355. [META-020] Counterfactuals Hold in Virtue of Dependence-Relations
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: All counterfactuals affirm the existence of instances of necessitation of some kind. Causal counterfactuals hold in virtue of causal dependence-relations; logical counterfactuals hold in virtue of logical dependence-relations.

356. [META-021] David Lewis's Possible Worlds Analysis of Counterfactuals Rejected
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: David Lewis's analysis of counterfactuals in terms of similar possible worlds is false. Events have deep causal roots such that changing one event requires either massive world-differences or changes to deterministic mechanisms.

357. [META-022] Program-Causality Distinguished from Token-Causality
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: Program-causality differs from token-causality. Sometimes the appropriate causal explanation cites a condition that guaranteed occurrence of token-causes rather than the token-causes themselves.

358. [META-023] Causal Redundancy Defeats Counterfactual Analysis
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: Where there is causal redundancy, causal truths cannot be converted into counterfactual truths. 'X caused Y' can be true even when 'if X hadn't occurred, Y wouldn't have occurred' is false.

359. [META-028] Necessary Truths Don't Describe the Empirical World
   Sources: Chapter Part 1, Section VIII
   Thesis: If proposition P is necessarily true, then P doesn't describe the empirical world in any way at all. P's being true in world W₁ doesn't distinguish W₁ from W₂ or any other possible world. A 'description' that fails to distinguish its object from other possible objects is no description at all.

360. [META-029] Transcendental Deduction of World-Structure from Coherent Experience
   Sources: Chapter Part 1, Section X
   Thesis: There is a hard-analytic connection between 'there exist beings who have cognitive wherewithal to raise metaphysical questions' and propositions about world-structure: any two events are related in space or time; there are causal relations among states of affairs; objects are things in which properties inhere (not mere bundles); human beings are things in which perceptions inhere (not mere bundles).

361. [META-030] To be at rest is to fail to move during a non-null interval; an object at a point during its motion is not at rest
   Sources: WORK-008 (Arrow Paradox)
   Thesis: To be at rest is to fail to move during a non-null interval; an object at a point during its motion is not at rest

362. [META-031] Any finite number can be represented as sum of infinite series; any case of motion involves traversing infinitely many finite distances in finite time
   Sources: WORK-008 (Zeno's Paradox)
   Thesis: Any finite number can be represented as sum of infinite series; any case of motion involves traversing infinitely many finite distances in finite time

363. [META-032] Space-time regions do not themselves have places; they are constituents, not occupants, of space-time
   Sources: WORK-008 (Paradox of Place)
   Thesis: Space-time regions do not themselves have places; they are constituents, not occupants, of space-time

364. [META-033] For x to cause y is for y to be a continuation of x, not for x-like events to always precede y-like events
   Sources: WORK-008 (Riddle of Induction)
   Thesis: For x to cause y is for y to be a continuation of x, not for x-like events to always precede y-like events

365. [META-034] Continuity represents preservation; discontinuity represents ex nihilo change
   Sources: WORK-008 (Riddle of Induction)
   Thesis: Continuity represents preservation; discontinuity represents ex nihilo change

366. [META-035] The legitimacy of inference from cause to effect lies in the irrationality of positing spontaneities
   Sources: WORK-008 (Riddle of Induction)
   Thesis: The legitimacy of inference from cause to effect lies in the irrationality of positing spontaneities

367. [META-036] Eternal matter vs. creation ex nihilo
   Sources: WORK-028 (lines 240-270)
   Thesis: There are two fundamentally different metaphysical pictures: (1) creation ex nihilo, where God creates being from non-being, making God absolutely primary and matter derivative; (2) eternal matter, where God organizes pre-existing matter, making God and matter coequal ultimates. These lead to radically different theologies.

368. [META-037] Pre-Civilizational Perception as Direct and Natural
   Sources: WORK-030 (lines 36-37, 164-166)
   Thesis: Pre-civilizational humans perceived agency and meaning in natural phenomena (lightning as divine wrath) directly and naturally, not through cultural interpretation or belief.

369. [META-038] Modal Realism's Ontological Extravagance
   Sources: WORK-031 (lines 71-74)
   Thesis: Modal realism requires accepting the concrete existence of infinitely many alternative universes, an ontological commitment many philosophers find unacceptably extravagant.

370. [META-039] Essentialism and Modal Knowledge
   Sources: WORK-031 (lines 2207-2255)
   Thesis: The doctrine that objects have essential properties (properties they must have to exist) creates special problems for modal analysis but may be unavoidable.

371. [META-040] Space-Time Structure and Modality
   Sources: WORK-031 (lines 24-26, 900-1200)
   Thesis: Questions about the structure of space-time (whether it's absolute or relational, whether space and time are unified) have implications for modal metaphysics.

372. [META-051] The universe is counter-entropic through life
   Sources: WORK-034
   Thesis: While the physical universe is entropic (disintegrating according to thermodynamics), biological reality is counter-entropic - organisms grow and assume increasingly sophisticated energy-efficient forms. This counter-entropic tendency is even stronger at the psychological level.

373. [META-052] Indeterminism prevents composite objects
   Sources: WORK-035
   Thesis: In an indeterministic universe, composite objects like billiard balls couldn't exist. The trillions of micro-particles would not be kept in place by deterministic laws and would go their own ways, causing the object to disintegrate or never form in the first place. Even atoms require deterministic laws among their parts to exist.

374. [META-053] States of affairs are causal processes
   Sources: WORK-037
   Thesis: It is part of our concept of a state of affairs (excluding completely instantaneous ones) that such things are causal sequences. States of affairs cannot be understood as non-causal. This undermines Humean attempts to define causation in terms of regularities among states of affairs.

375. [META-054] Temporal priority defined causally
   Sources: WORK-037
   Thesis: Temporal succession is itself a causal notion. A standard definition: x precedes y iff some possible (or actual) causal sequence connects x to y. Given that forces are deformations of space-time and no two events are totally causally isolated, x precedes y iff there is some causal process starting with x that ends with y.

376. [META-41] Truth is discovered, not made
   Sources: WORK-032 (lines 277-278, 334)
   Thesis: The pragmatist thesis (v) that 'truth is made, not discovered' is false. The negation (vN) 'truth is discovered, not made' is correct. Truth is objective—either there is an even number of stars in the galaxy or there isn't, independently of human construction.

377. [META-42] Knowledge is a subject-object relationship; truth is objective
   Sources: WORK-032 (lines 356-361)
   Thesis: Truth is objective—it concerns facts about the world independent of knowers. But knowledge is a relationship between subject and object, between mind and world, and therefore has a subjective side. Speaking biologically, the intellect exists to help the organism survive and procreate, though in humans it has become partly autonomous.

378. [METH-005] Cell Biology Analogy Supports Collective-First Methodology
   Sources: Paragraphs 5-6
   Thesis: Just as individual cells are better understood in terms of their role in multicellular organisms rather than as autonomous individuals pursuing their own survival, individual humans are better understood in terms of their roles in human collectives. Cell-individualism can be explained in terms of cell-collectivism, but not vice versa; similarly for person-individualism and person-collectivism.

379. [METH-006] Collective Behavior Cannot Be Explained by Individualist Assumptions
   Sources: Paragraph 8
   Thesis: It is very difficult to explain group behavior - whether mobs or advanced cultures - on the assumption that collectives represent averages of behaviors of autonomous individual deciders. Outside of contexts with immediate physical threats or prospects of crude gratification, people give far too much weight to existing beliefs and mores in far too well-defined ways to be accounted for by individualist hypotheses.

380. [METH-007] AI Construction Tests Cognitive Theories Empirically
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 4: Reverse Brain Engineering)
   Thesis: The requirement that cognitive theories be implementable in functioning AI systems provides an empirical constraint: theories that cannot generate working systems are falsified. This connects abstract theorizing to concrete engineering requirements.

381. [METH-008] Formalization Requires Generative Not Just Organizational Adequacy
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 10: Rethinking Formalization)
   Thesis: AI reveals that adequate formalization requires not just organizing existing knowledge but generating new valid instances. A formalization is adequate only if it can generate the full range of valid cases, not merely classify them post hoc.

382. [METH-009] Generative Tests Reveal Hidden Inadequacies in Formalizations
   Sources: WORK-011 (Essay 10)
   Thesis: Testing whether a formalization can generate valid new instances (as AI systems must) reveals inadequacies invisible to purely organizational approaches. Many traditional formalizations fail this stronger generative test.

383. [METH-010] No Such Thing as Enumerative Induction
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: There is no such thing as enumerative induction (inferring 'all Fs are Gs' from 'all observed Fs are Gs'). What appears to be enumerative induction always involves implicit appeal to Inference to the Best Explanation (IBE).

384. [METH-011] Discontinuity-Elimination the Essence of Explanation
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: To explain is to eliminate discontinuities. A correct scientific hypothesis eliminates anomalies by showing how apparently disconnected facts are part of continuous causal processes.

385. [METH-012] Inductive Knowledge as Knowledge of Continuities
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: Inductive knowledge is knowledge of continuities between continuities. Valid induction extends known continuous causal processes to unobserved cases by identifying higher-order patterns of continuity.

386. [METH-013] IBE Justified by Continuity-Completion
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: Inference to the Best Explanation (IBE) is justified because it completes causal continuities. A hypothesis is justified to the extent it fills gaps in causal sequences, eliminating discontinuities in the data.

387. [METH-014] Measurement Distinct from Enumeration
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: Measurement is fundamentally distinct from enumeration. Length cannot be determined by counting because (1) there are no minimal length units, and (2) length is not strictly additive at large scales.

388. [METH-015] Relativization to Standards the Essence of Measurement
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: The essence of measurement is comparison with respect to a standard. All measurement, including 'absolute' measurement, is fundamentally relative comparison to stipulated standards.

389. [METH-016] Three Axioms of Measurement Theory
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: Three axioms are necessary for any viable measurement system: (i) If L1>L2 and L2>L3, then L1>L3 (transitivity). (ii) If L1≠L2, one is greater (trichotomy). (iii) If L1=L2 and L2=L3, then L1=L3 (transitivity of identity). Violation makes measurement meaningless.

390. [METH-017] Metrical Conventionalism Versus Strict Conventionalism
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: Conventionalism (laws are convention-based) differs from strict conventionalism (laws are convention-determined). Only the former is correct. That laws are expressed relative to conventional standards doesn't make them entirely conventional.

391. [METH-018] Conventionalism Self-Defeating
   Sources: WORK-014
   Thesis: Conventionalism is self-defeating. If laws hold in virtue of non-conventional relations between conventions and events (as conventionalism requires), then measurement standard choice is constrained by objective facts, undermining conventionalism.

392. [METH-022] Effective Therapy Requires Purpose-Oriented Reengagement
   Sources: Lines 386-391
   Thesis: The way to beat OCD is to have a purpose in life and choose growth over mere freedom from anxiety. Cognitive-behavioral therapy has shallow, short-lived effects absent determination to grow and change in ways that make OCD irrelevant.

393. [METH-023] The #1 Rule of Writing is that one must immediately write down every thought that occurs
   Sources: WORK-026 (The #1 Rule of Writing)
   Thesis: The #1 Rule of Writing is that one must immediately write down every thought that occurs

394. [METH-024] If one waits to write down a thought, the moment will pass and never be recovered
   Sources: WORK-026 (The #1 Rule of Writing)
   Thesis: If one waits to write down a thought, the moment will pass and never be recovered

395. [METH-025] The idea that originally comes to mind in writing is merely a foil for a constellation of unconscious ideas
   Sources: WORK-026 (The #1 Rule of Writing)
   Thesis: The idea that originally comes to mind in writing is merely a foil for a constellation of unconscious ideas

396. [METH-026] Following the immediate-writing injunction makes one ipso facto an honest writer
   Sources: WORK-026 (The #1 Rule of Writing)
   Thesis: Following the immediate-writing injunction makes one ipso facto an honest writer

397. [METH-027] Complete honesty is the #1 rule of writing (compatible with the immediate-writing rule)
   Sources: WORK-026 (The #1 Rule of Writing)
   Thesis: Complete honesty is the #1 rule of writing (compatible with the immediate-writing rule)

398. [METH-028] In business, one must do what comes naturally and not go against one's own grain
   Sources: WORK-026 (The #1 Rule of Business)
   Thesis: In business, one must do what comes naturally and not go against one's own grain

399. [METH-029] One must work hard, but not work hard fighting one's own nature
   Sources: WORK-026 (The #1 Rule of Business)
   Thesis: One must work hard, but not work hard fighting one's own nature

400. [METH-030] Mind-cure (hypnosis-based precursor of psychoanalysis) has effects similar to religious conversion
   Sources: WORK-024 (VRE Lectures IV-VII summary)
   Thesis: Mind-cure (hypnosis-based precursor of psychoanalysis) has effects similar to religious conversion

401. [METH-031] Mind-cure is effective because it relaxes the conscious-unconscious boundary, mending internal divisions
   Sources: WORK-024 (VRE Lectures IV-VII summary)
   Thesis: Mind-cure is effective because it relaxes the conscious-unconscious boundary, mending internal divisions

402. [METH-032] Psychoanalysis lowers the consciousness-unconsciousness barrier gradually, so emotional changes happen correspondingly slowly
   Sources: WORK-024 (Analysis section)
   Thesis: Psychoanalysis lowers the consciousness-unconsciousness barrier gradually, so emotional changes happen correspondingly slowly

403. [METH-033] Recovering from alcoholism tends to involve helping other alcoholics recover
   Sources: WORK-024 (Introduction - BB contentions)
   Thesis: Recovering from alcoholism tends to involve helping other alcoholics recover

404. [METH-034] Wittgenstein's and Quine's paradoxes are motivated by behaviorist psychological theories and, though meant to support such theories, actually presuppose them and show how broken they are
   Sources: WORK-008 (Wittgenstein's Rule-following Paradox)
   Thesis: Wittgenstein's and Quine's paradoxes are motivated by behaviorist psychological theories and, though meant to support such theories, actually presuppose them and show how broken they are

405. [METH-035] Philosophers respond defensively to neuroscience challenges
   Sources: WORK-027 (lines 1800-1850)
   Thesis: When faced with neuroscientific challenges to cherished philosophical positions (like free will), philosophers tend to respond defensively by dismissing the science, raising standards of proof to impossible levels, or claiming the empirical findings are irrelevant. This represents intellectual defensiveness rather than genuine engagement.

406. [METH-036] Religious thought prioritizes function over logical consistency
   Sources: WORK-028 (lines 650-710)
   Thesis: Smith's theological method prioritizes the functional religious question (What must God be like for the right divine-human relationship?) over logical consistency and philosophical rigor. This represents a pragmatic rather than rationalistic approach to theology, where practical religious adequacy matters more than abstract coherence.

407. [METH-037] Levels of Analysis in Studying Phenomena
   Sources: WORK-030 (lines 44-49)
   Thesis: Phenomena like religion admit of both reductive (causal-explanatory) and non-reductive (phenomenological-essential) analysis, both of which may provide genuine truths.

408. [METH-038] Statement vs. Analysis in Philosophy
   Sources: WORK-031 (lines 94-99)
   Thesis: Many philosophical claims are valuable as true statements while failing as analyses; confusing these roles leads to philosophical errors.

409. [METH-039] Limits of Conceptual Analysis
   Sources: WORK-031 (lines Throughout circularity arguments)
   Thesis: Attempts to analyze all concepts in terms of more basic ones eventually fail; some concepts must be accepted as primitive and understood through their systematic interrelations.

410. [METH-040] Theoretical Virtues and Theory Choice
   Sources: WORK-031 (lines Throughout, implicit in criticisms)
